STATISTICAL TEST GLOSSARY
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  Abbreviation    Full Name                       What It Measures
  --------------  ------------------------------  --------------------------------------------------
  KS stat         Kolmogorov-Smirnov statistic    Maximum vertical distance between the two CDFs (0 = identical, 1 = completely separate)
  KS p            KS p-value                      Probability of observing this difference by chance under H0 (same distribution)
  MW p            Mann-Whitney U p-value          Non-parametric test for whether one distribution tends to have larger values than the other
  Wass            Wasserstein distance            How much work to transform one distribution into the other — scale-sensitive, unlike KS
  Sig             Significance marker             ns = not significant (p >= 0.05);  * = p < 0.05;  ** = p < 0.01  (based on KS p-value)
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

COMMENTARY — Interpretation of Table 4
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Tortuosity Analysis: The mean tortuosity of crack edges is nearly identical between synthetic and
real-world masks (synthetic: 1.526 +/- 0.555; real-world: 1.570 +/- 0.648). Real-world edges exhibit
marginally higher tortuosity (1.570 vs. 1.526), consistent with the irregular propagation patterns
found in physical fractures. The difference is statistically significant (KS p = 0.0000), indicating
a detectable distributional gap.

Curvature Analysis: Local curvature statistics are notably different between the two datasets (mean
curvature — synthetic: 0.1381 +/- 0.3185, real-world: 0.3342 +/- 3.1464; curvature variance —
synthetic: 84.9018 +/- 3090.2807, real-world: 5957.4946 +/- 99090.4476). Real-world edges exhibit
higher mean curvature (0.3342 vs. 0.1381), consistent with the rougher, more irregular boundaries
characteristic of physically propagated cracks. Mean curvature distributions are significantly
different (KS p = 0.0000), and curvature variance distributions are significantly different (KS p =
0.0000).

Statistical Overview: Of the 9 features tested, 9 showed statistically significant distributional
differences, suggesting that while the synthetic cracks capture the general morphological character
of real-world cracks, systematic differences in edge geometry remain. The largest Wasserstein
distance was observed for 'Curvature Var - Std' (24077.1475), indicating this feature has the
greatest distributional separation, while 'Curvature Var - Median' showed the smallest distance
(0.0088), confirming near-identical distributions for that feature.

Nearest-Neighbour Matching: Pairwise NN matching in the normalised feature space (tortuosity mean,
curvature mean, curvature variance mean) yielded a mean distance of 0.0123 (median: 0.0048, std:
0.0646). These distances are very close, indicating that for the majority of synthetic images, a
geometrically similar real-world counterpart exists in the dataset. The relatively low standard
deviation (0.0646) indicates consistent similarity across the synthetic dataset, rather than a few
outlier synthetic images driving the overall result.

Overall Conclusion: The combined evidence from tortuosity analysis, curvature statistics,
distribution-level hypothesis tests, and pairwise nearest-neighbour matching indicates partial
geometric correspondence between synthetic and real-world crack boundaries. Further refinement of
the synthetic generation parameters — particularly those governing edge irregularity and curvature —
may be warranted to improve fidelity.
